The Dual Nature of a Slave and Its Effect on Jinaayah
Explaining the two shabahs (humanity and property) that govern a slave's jinaayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling for a slave's jinaayah based on the dual aspects of humanity and property, and compare it with the views of Imam Shafi'i and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A slave is both human and property; the Hanafi ruling gives effect to both aspects by allowing the master to choose between handing over the slave or paying the arsh.

This passage delves into the legal implications of a slave's dual nature—humanity (adamity) and property (maliyyah). These two aspects carry distinct rulings that normally conflict when combined in a single entity. The Hanafi school resolves this conflict by giving each aspect its due weight, resulting in a balanced ruling for the master's liability. Understanding this approach not only clarifies the slave's jinaayah but also illustrates the Hanafi method of harmonizing conflicting principles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding the master's options when his slave injures another. Note that the master may choose to hand over the slave (taslim al-raqabah) or pay the arsh (fida), but if he pays the full value, the slave's body transfers to the victim.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Shafi'i holds that the master must pay the arsh and cannot hand over the slave. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the master must hand over the slave and cannot pay the arsh.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Each opponent relies on one of the two shabahs: Shafi'i on humanity (no division, no ownership of body), Sahibayn on property (division, ownership of body). They consider only one aspect as decisive.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that both aspects are present and must be given effect. Neither can be ignored. Hence the master is given a choice, which respects both shabahs.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Shafi'i, the master cannot hand over the slave; under Sahibayn, the master must hand over; under Hanafi, the master has flexibility. This difference affects how liability is settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آدمیت اور مالیت کے جدا جدا احکام ہیں
ثم من أحكام الأولى أن لا ينقسم على الأجزاء ولا يتملك الجثة ومن أحكام الثانية أن ينقسم ويتملك الجثة فوفرنا على الشبهين حظهما من الحكم
ترجمہ پھر اوّل ( آدمیت) کے احکام میں سے یہ ہے کہ موجب جنایت اجزاء پر منقسم نہ ہو اور وہ جستہ کا مالک نہ ہو اور ثانی (مالیت) کے احکام میں سے یہ ہے کہ منقسم ہو جائے اور جانی بشہ کا مالک ہو جائے تو ہم نے ہر دومشابہتوں پر ہر ایک کا حکم پھیلا دیا۔ تشریح آدمیت اور مالیت کے جدا جدا احکام ہیں مثلاً ا آدمیت کا حکم یہ ہے کہ ضمان اجزاء پر منقسم نہ ہوگا۔ لہذا جب آزاد کی آنکھیں چھوڑی گئیں تو وہاں یہ حکم نہ ہوگا کہ آنکھوں اور باقی بدن میں حساب لگا کر فانی کا ضمان واجب کر دو اور باقی بدن کے بقد رضمان واجب نہ ہو۔ -۲- آدمیت کا دوسرا حکم یہ ہے کہ ادا ضمان کے بعد مجرم بشہ کا مالک نہیں ہو گا۔ اور مالیت کا حکم یہ ہے کہ مضمان اجزاء پر منقسم ہوگا۔ لہذا اگر کپڑا پچھاڑ گیا تو جتنا نقصان ہے وہ وصول کر لیا جائے اور کپڑا مالک کے پاس رہنے دیا جائے اور اگر پور اضمان (قیمت وصول کر لیا گیا تو کپڑا جانی کے حوالہ کیا جائے ، بہر حال آدمیت اور مالیت کے احکام الگ ہیں اور غلام میں ان دونوں حیثیتوں کا اجتماع ہے لہذا دونوں کا لحاظ ضروری ہے تا کہ دونوں مشابہتوں پر عمل ہو سکے۔ اور امام شافعی نے جو فرمایا ہے اس میں مالیت کے پہلو کو بالکل لغو قرار دینا ہے، اور آدمیت کی جانب کا پورا لحاظ ہے اور صاحبین کے فرمان میں آدمیت کا بالکل ایفاء اور مالیت کا پورا اعتبار ہے اور اعدل قول امام صاحب کا ہے جس میں دونوں مشابہتوں پر پورا پورا عمل کیا گیا ہے۔ لہذا آدمیت کے لحاظ سے ہم نے کہا کہ مالک کو یہ حق نہ ہوگا کہ پورا بدل ( قیمت ) لے کر پھر بھی غلام کو اپنے پاس رکھے۔ اس کے مالک کو یہ حق ہوگا۔ بلکہ پوری قیمت لینے کی صورت میں غلام جانی کے حوالہ کرنا ہو گا ۔
The rulings of humanity and property are separate. Then from the rulings of the first (humanity) is that it does not divide upon parts nor does one own the body; and from the rulings of the second (property) is that it divides and one owns the body. So we have given each of the two semblances its share of the ruling. Translation: Then from the rulings of the first (humanity) is that the liability for injury does not divide upon parts and the injured does not own the body; and from the rulings of the second (property) is that it divides and the injured owns the body. So we have spread the ruling upon each of the two semblances. Explanation: The rulings of humanity and property are separate. For example, one ruling of humanity is that guarantee does not divide upon parts. Therefore when a free person's eyes are taken, the ruling is not that one calculates between the eyes and the rest of the body, making the arsh of the eyes wajib and the rest not. Second ruling of humanity is that after payment of the guarantee, the criminal does not become the owner of the body. And the ruling of property is that guarantee divides upon parts. For example, if a cloth is torn, the extent of damage is recovered and the cloth remains with the owner; and if full guarantee (value) is recovered, the cloth is handed over to the tortfeasor. All in all, the rulings of humanity and property are separate, and in a slave, both these aspects combine. Therefore both must be considered so that both semblances are acted upon. Imam Shafi'i's statement completely disregards the property aspect and fully considers the humanity aspect. The statement of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) completely disregards humanity and fully considers property. And the most balanced statement is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, in which both semblances are fully acted upon. Therefore, from the perspective of humanity, we said that the owner does not have the right to take the full compensation (price) and still keep the slave. He does have the right, but in the case of taking full price, the slave must be handed over to the tortfeasor.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage discusses the basis of the Hanafi ruling for a full slave's jinaayah. The author explains that a slave possesses two aspects: humanity (adamity) and property (maliyyah). The rulings of each are distinct. Humanity prevents division of liability and ownership of the criminal's body, while property allows division and transfer of ownership. In a slave, both combine, so the law gives weight to both. This leads to the rule that for a slave's injury, the master can either hand over the slave or pay the arsh. If he pays the full value, he cannot also keep the slave. The passage then contrasts Imam Shafi'i and the Sahibayn: Shafi'i focuses only on humanity, so he rules that the master must pay the arsh and cannot hand over the slave; the Sahibayn focus only on property, so they rule that the master must hand over the slave and cannot pay the arsh. Imam Abu Hanifa's position is balanced, giving effect to both aspects, allowing the master the choice between handing over or paying, but with the condition that if he pays the full value, he must also hand over the slave.

Key Terms
شبه (shabah): In this context, shabah refers to a 'semblance' or 'aspect' of a legal entity. A slave has two shabahs: one resembling a free human (adamity) and one resembling property (maliyyah). The ruling for the slave is derived by giving each shabah its due weight.
آدمیت (adamity): Humanity; the quality of being a human. In this passage, it is the aspect of a slave that is like a free human, giving rise to rulings that do not allow division of liability or transfer of bodily ownership.
مالیت (maliyyah): Property; the quality of being owned and having monetary value. In this passage, it is the aspect of a slave that is like property, allowing division of liability and transfer of bodily ownership upon payment of full value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what options does a master have when his slave commits a jinaayah (injury) on another slave?
A) Only hand over the slave
B) Only pay the arsh
C) Either hand over the slave or pay the arsh, but if he pays full value, the slave transfers
D) Neither; the master is not liable

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on a slave's jinaayah balances the slave's dual nature as human and property, giving the master the option to hand over the slave or pay the arsh, with the caveat that full payment transfers ownership.

Liability for Mudabbar and Umm Walad's Injury: Lesser of Value or Arsh
Ruling with proof for mudabbar and umm walad injury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a mudabbar's or umm walad's injury and explain why the master must pay the lesser of the slave's value and the arsh.

Core Idea
For mudabbar and umm walad, the master pays the lesser of value and arsh – no option to hand over.

This lesson focuses on a special category of slaves: the mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death) and the umm walad (mother of the master's child). Their unique status changes the rules of liability when they commit injuries. Unlike a full slave, the master is not given a choice to hand them over; instead, he must pay the lesser amount. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the laws of diyat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Recognize that the injurer is a mudabbar or umm walad, and the act is a non-lethal injury having a specific arsh.
2
State the ruling
The master is liable for the lesser of the slave's current market value and the arsh of the injury.
This is a wajib payment, not an option.
3
Understand the rationale
The mudabbar and umm walad cannot be handed over due to their special status (tadbir/istilad creates an impediment). The master is like one who, unaware of the injury, freed a slave – in both cases, the master pays the lesser.
The rationale is that the master prevented handover without fault.
4
Identify the proof
The proof is the judgment of Abu Ubaydah ibn al-Jarrah, which was accepted by the Companions, forming consensus. Also, qiyas to a freed slave after injury.
The consensus strengthens the Hanafi position.
5
Compare with a full slave (qinn)
For a qinn, the master has the option to hand over the slave or pay the arsh. For mudabbar/umm walad, no option exists because the slave cannot be handed over.
The option is beneficial for the full slave because the victim may prefer the slave over monetary compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في جناية المدبّر وام الولد
ترجمہ ی فصل ہے مدبر اور ام ولد کی جنایت کے بیان میں
كتاب الديات
تشریح مدبر اور ام ولد کا درجہ رقیت میں غلام سے ناقص ہے اس لئے ان کا بیان غلام کی جنایت کے بعد کیا گیا ہے۔ مدبر اور ام ولد نے کوئی جنایت کی تو آقا اس کی قیمت اور جنایت کے ارش میں سے اقل کا ضامن ہوگا
قال وإذا جنى المدير وأم الولد جنابة ضمن المولى الأقل من قيمته ومن أرشها لما روى عن أبي عبيدة رضى الله عنه أنه قضى بجناية المدبر على مولاه ولأنه صار مانعا عن تسليمه فى الجناية بالتدبير أو الاستيلاد من غير اختياره الفداء فصار كما إذا فعل ذلك بعد الجناية وهو لا يعلم ترجمہ اور جب کہ مدبر اور ام ولد نے کوئی جنایت کی تو آقا اس کی قیمت اور جنایت کے ارش میں سے اقل کا ضامن ہوگا ، بوجہ اس کے کہ ابو عبیدہ سے مروی ہے کہ انہوں نے مدبر کی جنایت کا فیصلہ اس کے آقا پر کیا ہے اور اس لئے کہ آقاء تد بیر یا استیلا د کی وجہ سے بغیر فدیہ اختیار کئے ہوئے جنایت کے اندر تسلیم سے مانع ہو گیا پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسے بغیر علم کے بعد جنایت اس نے یہ کام کیا ہو۔ تشریح ماقبل میں مسئلہ گزر چکا ہے کہ اگر غلام نے جنایت کی لیکن آقا کو اس کی جنایت کا علم نہیں : وسکا تھا اسی حالت میں آقا نے غلام کو آزاد کر دیا تو یہاں آقا کو فدیہ اختیار کرنے والا شمار نہ کیا جائے گا لیکن چونکہ مجنی علیہ کا بھی کچھ تصور نہیں ہے اس وجہ سے یہاں آقا پر واجب ہے کہ جنایت کا ارش اور غلام کی قیمت میں سے جو کم ہو وہ دیدے کیونکہ غلام آزاد کرنے کے بعد اب اس کے دینے کا تو سوال ختم ہو چکا ہے۔
بالکل اسی طرح یہاں کا مسئلہ ہے کہ مدبر اور ام ولد جنایت دیے جانے کے قابل نہیں ہیں مگر آقا کو کیا خبرتھی کہ یہ آگے یوں کریں گے، لہذا یہاں بھی اگر انہوں نے کوئی جنایت کی یہی حکم ہوگا کہ ان کی قیمت اور ارش میں سے جو کم ہو وہ دیدیا جائے اب رہی یہ بات کہ مدبر کی جنایت کا عمان آقا پر کیوں ہوا ہے تو اس باب میں اگر چہ حدیث تو نہیں ہے لیکن حضرت ابو عبیدہ ابن الجراح شام کے امیر تھے انہوں نے مدبر کی جنایت کا ضمان اس کے آقا پر واجب کیا تھا اور بہت سے صحابہ کی موجودگی میں کیا تھا اور کسی صحابی نے اس پر نکیر نہیں کی تو گویا اس پر اجماع ہو گیا۔
قیمت اور ارش میں اقل واجب ہوگا
وإنما يجب الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش لأنه لا حق لولى الجناية فى أكثر من الأرش ولا منع من المولى في أكثر من القيمة ولا تخيير بين الأقل والأكثر لأنه لايفيد فى جنس واحد لاختياره الأقل لامحالة بخلاف القن لأن الرغبات صادقة في الأعيان فيفيد التخيير بين الدفع والفداء.
ترجمہ اور اس کی قیمت اور ارش سے اقل واجب ہوگا اس لئے کہ ولی جنایت کا ارش سے زیادہ میں کوئی حق نہیں ہے اور آقا کی جانب سے قیمت سے تخیر غیر مفید ہے اس کے یقینا اقل کو اختیار کرنے کی وجہ سے بخلاف فن کے اس لئے کہ اعیان میں رغبات صادق ہیں تو دفع
Chapter on the injury of a mudabbar and umm walad. Translation: This is a chapter on the statement of the injury of a mudabbar and umm walad. Explanation: The status of a mudabbar and umm walad in terms of bondage is inferior to a slave; therefore their statement is made after the injury of a slave. If a mudabbar and umm walad commit an injury, the master is the guarantor of the lesser of its value and the arsh of the injury. He said: And if the mudabbar and umm walad commit an injury, the master becomes the guarantor of the lesser of its value and its arsh. Because it is narrated from Abu Ubaydah, may Allah be pleased with him, that he judged the injury of a mudabbar against his master. And because the master, by tadbir or istilad, has become a preventer of handing him over in the injury without choosing the ransom, so it is as if he did that after the injury while he did not know. Translation: When a mudabbar and umm walad commit an injury, the master will be the guarantor of the lesser of its value and the arsh of the injury. Because of what is narrated from Abu Ubaydah that he judged the injury of a mudabbar against his master. And because the master, due to tadbir or istilad, without choosing ransom, became a preventer of handing over in the injury, so it is as if he did that act after the injury while he was unaware. Explanation: In the previous passage, the issue passed that if a slave committed an injury but the master had no knowledge of the injury, and in that state the master freed the slave, then here the master will not be counted as one who chooses ransom. But because the victim also has some consideration, therefore upon the master it is wajib that the arsh of the injury and the value of the slave, whichever is less, he pay, because after freeing the slave, the question of handing him over is finished. Exactly similarly is the issue here that a mudabbar and umm walad are not fit to be handed over for injury, but what knowledge did the master have that they would do this in the future. Therefore here too if they commit an injury, the same ruling applies: whichever is less between their value and arsh should be paid. Now the remaining matter, why the guarantee of the mudabbar's injury is on the master, so in this chapter, although there is no hadith, but Sayyidina Abu Ubaydah ibn al-Jarrah, who was the amir of Syria, he made the guarantee of the mudabbar's injury wajib upon his master. And he did this in the presence of many Companions, and no Companion denied it, so it was as if consensus was reached. The lesser of value and arsh will be wajib. And the lesser of its value and arsh is wajib because the guardian of the injury has no right to more than the arsh, and from the master's side, there is no prevention of more than the value. And there is no option between the lesser and the greater because it does not benefit in one type, because he will inevitably choose the lesser. Unlike a full slave (qinn), because desires are sincere in objects, so option between handing over and ransom is beneficial. Translation: And the lesser of its value and arsh is wajib because the guardian of the injury has no right to more than the arsh, and from the master, there is no prevention of more than the value. And there is no option between the lesser and greater because it is not beneficial in one type due to his inevitably choosing the lesser. Unlike a qinn, because desires are sincere in objects, so the option between handing over and ransom is beneficial.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This lesson explains the ruling for a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is promised upon the master's death) and an umm walad (a slave mother of the master's child). Their status is inferior to a full slave in terms of property. When either commits an injury, the master is not given the option to hand over the slave or pay the arsh. Instead, the master must pay the lesser of the slave's market value and the injury's arsh. This is because they cannot be handed over due to their special status (tadbir and istilad create an impediment). The proof comes from the action of Abu Ubaydah ibn al-Jarrah, who ruled that a mudabbar's injury is borne by the master, and this was accepted by the Companions, amounting to consensus. Additionally, qiyas is made with the case where a master frees his slave after the injury without knowing about it – in that case too, the master pays the lesser value because the slave can no longer be handed over. The reason why the lesser is taken is because the victim has no right to more than the arsh, and the master cannot be forced to pay more than the slave's value. There is no option for the master to pay more or less; he must pay the lesser. This contrasts with a full slave (qinn), where the master has an option between handing over or paying, because the victim has interest in receiving the slave as compensation.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom by the master upon the master's death (tadbir). Such a slave has a special status, lower than a full slave in terms of property.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master. She becomes free upon the master's death and has a special status.
أرش (arsh): The compensation (blood money) for a non-lethal injury, such as for loss of a limb. It is a specified amount based on the body part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudabbar commits an injury that has an arsh of 500 dirhams, and his market value is 300 dirhams, what amount must the master pay?
A) 500 dirhams
B) 300 dirhams
C) 200 dirhams (difference)
D) The master can choose to hand over the mudabbar instead

Key Takeaway
For mudabbar and umm walad, the master's liability is limited to the lesser of the slave's value and the arsh, with no option to hand over the slave, based on the consensus of the Companions.

Master's Liability for Mudabbar or Umm al-Walad's Crime
States ruling and proof that master pays lesser of value and arsh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mudabbar or umm al-walad commits a crime and explain why the master pays the lesser of value and arsh, using the Hanafi proof and analogy.

Core Idea
Master pays the lesser of slave's value and arsh – no choice, because both are money.

This passage from Al-Hidayah explains a specific rule in Hanafi fiqh: the liability of a master for crimes committed by a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) or an umm al-walad (a slave who has borne the master's child). The ruling is not arbitrary; it is built on a narration from a Companion and a careful analogy. Understanding this ruling requires not only knowing the outcome but also the proof structure and the rationale behind the absence of a choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The master's liability when his mudabbar or umm al-walad commits a crime causing arsh. The ruling: the master must pay the lesser of the slave's market value and the arsh.
The slave is not fully free during the master's life, so the master retains ownership.
2
Locate the primary proof (dalil naqli)
The text cites the practice of Abu 'Ubaydah ibn al-Jarrah, who judged that the master bears the crime of a mudabbar. This happened with Companions present and no objection, forming an implicit consensus (ijma sukuti).
This is a qawl sahabi, which Hanafis consider authoritative unless contradicted.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogy) argument
The master, by performing tadbir or istilad, prevents surrendering the slave for the crime. However, he did not have the option to pay a ransom because he was unaware of the crime. This is similar to a case where the master prevents surrender after the crime but without knowledge – in that case too, he must pay the lesser. Thus, the illa (effective cause) is prevention of surrender without the option to ransom due to ignorance.
The analogy links the mudabbar/umm al-walad case to a known scenario from the madhhab.
4
Understand why no takhyeer (choice) is given
The text explains that the master cannot choose between paying the lesser or the greater because both are money (mal). Inevitably he would choose the lesser, making the choice useless. In contrast, with an ordinary slave (qinn), the master can choose to hand over the slave or pay ransom because these are different categories (physical object vs money), so choice is meaningful.
The distinction hinges on the genus of the two options: same genus (mal) vs different genus (ayn vs mal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإذا جنى المدبر وأم الولد جناية ضمن المولى الأقل من قيمته ومن أرشها لما روى عن أبي عبيدة رضى الله عنه أنه قضى بجناية المدبر على مولاه ولأنه صار مانعا عن تسليمه فى الجناية بالتدبير أو الاستيلاد من غير اختياره الفداء فصار كما إذا فعل ذلك بعد الجناية وهو لا يعلم. وإنما يجب الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش لأنه لا حق لولى الجناية فى أكثر من الأرش ولا منع من المولى في أكثر من القيمة ولا تخيير بين الأقل والأكثر لأنه لايفيد فى جنس واحد لاختياره الأقل لامحالة بخلاف القن لأن الرغبات صادقة في الأعيان فيفيد التخيير بين الدفع والفداء.
He said: When the mudabbar or umm al-walad commits a crime, the master is liable for the lesser of its value and its blood money, due to what was narrated from Abu 'Ubaydah, may Allah be pleased with him, that he ruled about the crime of a mudabbar against his master. And because he (the master) has prevented surrendering him (the slave) due to the tadbir or istilad, without choosing the ransom, so it becomes like when he did that after the crime without knowing. And the lesser of its value and the blood money is obligatory, because the guardian of the crime has no right to more than the blood money, and there is no prevention from the master regarding more than the value. And there is no choice between the lesser and the greater because it does not benefit in a single type (jins) since he will inevitably choose the lesser, unlike the qinn, because desires are genuine regarding physical objects, so choice between handing over and ransom is beneficial.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on offenses of the mudabbar and umm al-walad. The author states the ruling: when a mudabbar (a slave who will become free upon the master's death) or an umm al-walad (a slave who has borne her master's child) commits a crime, the master is liable to pay the lesser of the slave's market value and the arsh (blood money) for the crime. The proof is twofold: (1) the practice of Abu 'Ubaydah ibn al-Jarrah, who as governor of Sham judged that the master must bear the crime of a mudabbar. Since this happened in the presence of many Companions without objection, it constitutes an implicit consensus (ijma sukuti). (2) Analogy (qiyas): the master, by the very act of tadbir (promising freedom) or istilad (having a child), prevents the surrender of the slave for the crime, without having the option to pay a ransom (because he did not know of the crime beforehand). Thus, the master is like someone who, after the crime but without knowledge, prevented the slave's surrender – in that case too, he pays the lesser of value and arsh. The passage then explains why the lesser is obligatory: the victim's guardian cannot claim more than the arsh, and the master cannot be forced to pay more than the value. There is no choice between the lesser and greater because both are money (same genus), so the master would necessarily choose the lesser; the choice would be pointless. This differs from the case of an ordinary slave (qinn), where the master can choose to hand over the slave or pay the ransom, because here the two options are different in nature (a physical slave vs. money), and the master's preference may vary, so the choice is meaningful.

Key Terms
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whom the master has promised to free upon the master's death (tadbir).
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has given birth to a child acknowledged as the master's, and thus becomes free upon the master's death.
الأرش (al-arsh): The blood money (diyah) for a crime causing injury or death, as assessed by the court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the wisdom behind making the master liable for the lesser of the mudabbar's value and the arsh, rather than giving him a choice?
A) Because the master is at fault for not controlling the mudabbar.
B) Because both the value and arsh are of the same type (mal), so the master would inevitably choose the lesser; thus the law imposes the lesser to avoid pointless choice.
C) Because the victim's guardian can demand either one.
D) Because the mudabbar's crime is light.

Key Takeaway
The master pays the lesser of the slave's value and the arsh because both are money, and the prevention of surrender leaves no effective choice.

Multiple Crimes by Mudabbar: Single Payment Shared Proportionately
Ruling on consecutive mudabbar crimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consecutive crimes by a mudabbar and explain why only one payment is due, applying the analogy to handing over the slave.

Core Idea
Only one payment for consecutive mudabbar crimes because liability is tied to one neck.

When a mudabbar commits multiple crimes, a key question arises: does the master owe compensation for each crime separately? The Hanafi school rules that only one payment is due, shared among the victims. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the proportional distribution method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Recognise that the mudabbar's multiple crimes trigger a single obligation on the master: one payment of the mudabbar's value.
2
Understand the effective cause (illa)
The master's prevention (man') is limited to one slave, so liability cannot multiply.
Analogy: handing over the slave is a once-only event.
3
Apply the qiyas (analogy)
Paying value is like handing over the slave; since the latter cannot recur, the former also cannot recur.
This is a clear qiyas jaliy.
4
Distribute the payment proportionally
Victims share according to the mudabbar's value at the time of each crime, because the prevention is realized at that moment.
Example: if values differ, shares are adjusted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و جنايات المدبر وإن توالت لا تجب إلا قيمة واحدة لأنه لا منع منه إلا في رقبة واحدة ولأن دفع القيمة كدفع العبد وذلك لا يتكرر فهذا كذلك ويتضاربون بالحصص فيها وتعتبر قيمته لكل واحد في حال الجناية عليه لأن المنع في هذا الوقت يتحقق.
And the crimes of the mudabbar, even if successive, do not obligate more than one value because there is no prevention from him except in one neck (single person). And because paying the value is like handing over the slave, and that is not repeated, so this is likewise. And they (the victims) share in it in proportion, and his value for each one is considered at the time of the crime against him because the prevention is realized at that time.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on multiple crimes by a mudabbar. The author states that even if the mudabbar commits several crimes one after another (e.g., injures three people), the master is only liable to pay one value (the market value of the mudabbar at the time of payment). The rationale is twofold: (1) The master's involvement is limited to one 'neck' (the mudabbar himself); he only prevents the surrender of that single person. (2) Paying the value is equivalent to handing over the slave, and handing over can only happen once. Therefore, only one payment is due. However, the victims share that payment proportionately based on the value of the mudabbar at the time each crime occurred. The example given in the Urdu commentary: if the mudabbar's value was 100 at the time of the first crime, 50 at the second, and 25 at the third, and the master pays 75, the shares are adjusted accordingly (first gets 4/7, second 2/7, third 1/7). This is because at each moment, the master's 'prevention' (man') exists relative to that victim's claim.

Key Terms
يتضاربون (yatadaraboon): They share proportionately (as partners) in the single payment.
المنع (al-man'): The master's prevention of surrendering the slave for the crime.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three separate values, one for each crime.
B) One total value, shared proportionately among the victims.
C) The arsh of the most serious injury.
D) Nothing, because the mudabbar is free from liability.

Key Takeaway
Consecutive crimes by a mudabbar only obligate one payment of the mudabbar's value, shared proportionally among victims, because the master's ability to prevent surrender is limited to one slave.

Single Value for Successive Crimes of a Mudabbar and Proportional Distribution
Ruling on mudabbar's successive crimes: single value and proportional shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that only one value is due for successive crimes of a mudabbar and calculate each victim's proportional share using the slave's value at the time of the crime.

Core Idea
Multiple crimes by a mudabbar incur only one payment of value, shared proportionally by victims.

This passage from the Kitab al-Diyat establishes a fundamental principle regarding crimes committed by a mudabbar. When a mudabbar commits multiple acts of aggression against different persons, the master's liability is limited to a single payment of the slave's value, not a separate payment for each crime. The victims share this single value in proportion to the slave's value at the moment each crime occurred. This ruling is derived by analogy to handing over the slave himself, which cannot be repeated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon: a mudabbar's successive crimes (jinayat) against multiple victims. The ruling is that only one value (qīmah) is obligatory upon the master.
2
Understand the Analogy (Qiyas)
The ruling is based on qiyas: paying the value is like handing over the slave (daf' al-'abd). Just as a slave cannot be handed over multiple times for multiple crimes, the value also cannot be paid more than once.
The illa (effective cause) is that the master's right of retention is only over a single slave body.
3
Examine the Proportional Sharing Rule
The victims (awliyā' al-jināyah) share the single value proportionally. The share of each is based on the value of the mudabbar at the precise time of the crime against that victim. This is because the master's 'retention' (mana') is actualized at that time.
If the slave's value fluctuates over time, the value at the time of each specific crime is used for that victim's share calculation.
4
Work Through the Example
Apply the rule to a concrete example: Zayd (value 100), Khalid (value 50), Sajid (value 25). The total value (175) forms the base. The master pays a single sum (e.g., 75) and each victim receives a fraction: Zayd 100/175, Khalid 50/175, Sajid 25/175.
The actual payment amount is determined by the judge based on current value, but the shares are fixed by the value at crime times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

و جنايات المدبر ان توالت لا توجب إلا قيمة واحدة لأنه لا منع منه إلا في رقبة واحدة ولأن دفع القيمة كدفع العبد وذلك لا يتكرر فهذا كذلك ويتضاربون بالحصص فيها وتعتبر قيمته لكل واحد في حال الجناية عليه لأن المنع في هذا الوقت يتحقق.
ترجمہ اور مدبر کی جنایات اگر چہ پے در پے ہوں صرف ایک ہی قیمت واجب کرتی ہیں اس لئے کہ مولی کی طرف سے روک ایک ہی رقبہ میں ہے اور اس لئے کہ قیمت دینا غلام کو دینے کے مثل ہے اور وہ ( غلام دینا ) مکرر نہیں ہے پس ایسے ہی ( یعنی قیمت دینا بھی مکرر نہ ہو گا ) اور شریک ہو جائیں گے مستحق قیمت میں حصہ رسد اور ہر ایک کے حق میں مدبر کی وہ قیمت معتبر ہوگی جو اس پر جنایت کے وقت تھی اس لئے کہ روک اس وقت متحقق ہوتی ہے۔

قال فإن جن جناية أخرى وقد دفع المولى القيمة إلى ولى الجناية بقضاء فلاشيء عليه لأنه مجبور على الدفع
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا پس اگر مدبر نے دوسری جنایت کی حالانکہ مولیٰ جنایت اولی کے ولی کو قاضی کے فیصلہ سے قیمت دے چکا ہے تو اس پر کچھ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ مولیٰ دینے پر مجبور ہے۔
تشریح اگر مد بر نے جنایت کی اور مولی نے اس کی قیمت وتی جنایت کو دیدی اور قاضی کے حکم سے دی اور مد بر نے دوسری جنایت اور کر دی تو اب مولی پر کوئی ضمان نہ ہو گا۔
قال وإن كان المولى دفع القيمة بغير قضاء فالولى بالخيار إن شاء اتبع المولى وإن شاء اتبع ولى الجناية وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا شيء على المولى لأنه حين دفع لم تكن الجناية الثانية موجودة فقد دفع كل الحق إلى مستحقه وصار كما إذا دفع بالقضاء
ترجمه قدوری نے فرمایا اور اگر مولیٰ نے بغیر قضاء قاضی کے قیمت دیدی ہو تو ولی جنایت کو اختیار ہے اگر چاہے مولی کا پیچھا کرے اور اگر چاہے ( پہلے ) ولی جنایت کا پیچھا کرے اور یہ ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ مولیٰ پر کچھ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ جب اس نے قیمت دی تو دوسری جنایت موجود نہیں تھی تو مولی نے پورا حق اس کے مستحق کی طرف دیدیا اور ایسا ہو گیا جیسے جب کہ اس سے قضاء قاضی سے دیا ہو۔

وألأبي حنيفة أن المولى جان بدفع حق ولى الجناية الثانية طوعا وولى الأولى ضامن بقبض حقه ظلما فيتخير
The Book of Diyat. And the crimes of the mudabbar, if they occur successively, do not necessitate except a single value (qīmah) because there is no right of retention (mana') except in a single slave body (raqabah), and because paying the value is like handing over the slave, and that (handing over the slave) is not repeated, so this (paying value) is likewise. And they (the victims) will share proportionally in that value, and the value of the mudabbar is considered for each one at the moment of the crime against him because the retention (by the master) is actualized at that time. Translation: And if the mudabbar's crimes, even if consecutive, only impose one single value, because the master's retention is in one single slave body, and because giving the value is like giving the slave, and that (giving the slave) is not repeated, so likewise (giving the value is not repeated). And they (the victims) become partners in the value according to their shares, and for each one, the value of the mudabbar at the time of the crime against him is considered because the retention is actualized at that time. Commentary: If the mudabbar repeatedly commits crimes and offenses, only one value will be obligatory upon the master, because if the criminal were eligible to be handed over, only one handing over would be obligatory. And giving the value is like giving the slave, so only one value becomes obligatory. Then the claimants (victims) will share in that value proportionally, and the value of the mudabbar at the time of each crime is considered for that claimant because the master's retention is found for him at that time. For example, if the mudabbar committed crimes against three persons: Zayd, Khalid, and Sajid. At the time of the crime against Zayd, his value is 100 rupees; at the time of the crime against Khalid, 50; and at the time of the crime against Sajid, 25. Due to price fluctuations, these differences occur. Now, when the master pays a value by order of the judge, they will share according to this calculation. For example, if he pays 75 rupees, then Zayd gets 100/175, Khalid 50/175, Sajid 25/175. In other words, whatever the value, it is divided into seven parts in the mentioned scenario: Zayd gets 4/7, Khalid 2/7, Sajid 1/7. Al-Quduri said: If he (the mudabbar) commits another crime while the master had already paid the value to the first victim by court order (bi-qadā'), then nothing is upon him (the master) because he is compelled to pay. Translation: Al-Quduri said: If the mudabbar commits another crime while the master had already paid the value to the first victim by the judge's decision, then nothing is upon him because the master is compelled to pay. Commentary: If the mudabbar committed a crime and the master paid its value to the first victim by the judge's order, and then the mudabbar commits a second crime, then no liability falls on the master. Al-Quduri said: If the master paid the value without a court order (bi-ghayri qadā'), then the victim (of the second crime) has the option: if he wishes, he may pursue the master; if he wishes, he may pursue the first victim. This is according to Abu Hanifa. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Nothing is upon the master because at the time of payment, the second crime did not exist, so the master paid the entire right to its rightful recipient, and it becomes as if he paid by court order. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And if the master paid the value without the judge's decree, then the second victim has the option: if he wishes, he may pursue the master; if he wishes, he may pursue the first victim. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn said: Nothing is upon the master because when he paid the value, the second crime did not exist. So the master paid the entire right to its rightful recipient, and this is like when he pays by court order. Commentary: And if the master paid the value without the judge's decision, then later the mudabbar committed a second crime, what happens? The Sahibayn said: The second victim will have the option: he may demand his right from the master or from the first victim. The proof of the Sahibayn: When the master paid the value to the first victim, the second crime did not exist, and the master fully paid the right to its rightful recipient, so his payment is like payment by court order. And for Abu Hanifa: The master wronged (the second victim) by paying the right of the second victim voluntarily, and the first victim is liable (ḍāmin) for having received his right unjustly (ẓulman), so (the second victim) may choose (whom to pursue).
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage begins with a ruling on the rights of victims when a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after the master's death) commits multiple successive crimes (jinayat). The master's liability is limited to a single payment of the slave's value, regardless of the number of crimes, because the master's right of retention is only over one slave body. This is analogous to handing over the slave himself—just as a slave cannot be handed over multiple times for multiple crimes, the value (qīmah) stands in place of the slave and thus is also paid only once. The victims share proportionally in this single value based on the value of the slave at the time each crime was committed. This ruling sets the general principle. The passage then addresses a specific scenario: if the master has already paid the value for the first crime, either by court order (qadā') or voluntarily, and later the mudabbar commits a second crime. If the payment was made by court order, the master is deemed compelled to pay, and he bears no further liability for the second crime; the second victim has no claim against him. However, if the payment was made voluntarily (without qadā'), there is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilāf). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the second victim has a choice (khiyār): he may either pursue the master for his right or pursue the first victim (who received the payment). Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the master wronged the second victim by voluntarily paying the value to the first victim, thereby prejudicing the second victim's claim; the first victim, in turn, is considered a guarantor (ḍāmin) for having received the payment unjustly. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the master bears no liability because at the time of payment the second crime did not exist, so the master fully discharged his obligation to the rightful recipient. Their view treats the voluntary payment like a court-ordered payment, absolving the master. The text presents both positions without explicit tarjīḥ, though the commentary notes the difference. This passage is an example of how the Hanafi school derives rules through analogy (qiyās) and how differences within the school (ikhtilāf) are resolved by weighing competing analogies and principles.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom by his master upon the master's death, conditional upon the death. The master retains rights over him during the master's lifetime.
جناية (jināyah): A crime or offense committed against a person, typically causing bodily harm or death, entailing a claim of diyat (blood money) or qisas.
قيمة (qīmah): The monetary value of the slave, assessed at the time of the crime, paid as compensation when the master chooses not to hand over the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, how many values are due when a mudabbar commits three successive crimes against three different people?
A) Three values, one for each crime.
B) Two values, one for the first two and one for the third.
C) Only one value, shared proportionally.
D) No value, because the mudabbar is destined for freedom.

Key Takeaway
Successive crimes by a mudabbar obligate only one payment of the slave's value, shared proportionally among victims based on the value at the time of each crime.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Effect of Voluntary Payment vs. Court-Ordered Payment for Mudabbar's Crimes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of voluntary vs. court-ordered payment for mudabbar's crimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a master's voluntary payment for a mudabbar's first crime (without court order) makes him liable for a second crime, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Voluntary payment vs. court-ordered payment: Abu Hanifa gives the second victim a choice; Sahibayn absolve the master.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) on a specific scenario: the master pays the value for a mudabbar's first crime without a court order, and then the mudabbar commits a second crime. Is the master liable for the second crime? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the second victim has a choice to pursue the master or the first victim. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the master has no liability, because at the time of payment the second crime did not exist. The passage provides both positions and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Disputed Issue
The scenario: master voluntarily pays value (without qada) for first crime; then mudabbar commits second crime. The issue: is the master liable for the second crime?
2
State the Two Positions
Abu Hanifa: second victim has option (takhyir) to claim from master or first victim. Sahibayn: no liability on master.
Both positions are from within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the Proofs (Dalil) of Each Side
Abu Hanifa: master wronged second victim by voluntarily paying away the only asset (the slave's value) that could satisfy the second claim; first victim became a guarantor (damin) for receiving payment unjustly. Sahibayn: at the time of payment, second crime did not exist, so master's payment was a full discharge of his obligation to the rightful recipient; voluntary payment is like court-ordered.
The key difference is whether the master's action is considered a wrong (jany) against the second victim.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa: second victim can choose to sue either the master (who must pay again but can then recover from first victim) or the first victim directly. Under Sahibayn: master is free; second victim must claim from the first victim (who received the value).
This affects the burden of litigation and the likelihood of recovery.
5
Note the Absence of Tarjih in the Text
The passage does not state which view is relied upon (mufta bihi). Later Hanafi works may prefer Sahibayn to protect the master and avoid multiplicity of claims.
As a student, you should be aware that both views have valid Hanafi bases.

The Book of Diyat. And the crimes of the mudabbar, if they occur successively, do not necessitate except a single value (qīmah) because there is no right of retention (mana') except in a single slave body (raqabah), and because paying the value is like handing over the slave, and that (handing over the slave) is not repeated, so this (paying value) is likewise. And they (the victims) will share proportionally in that value, and the value of the mudabbar is considered for each one at the moment of the crime against him because the retention (by the master) is actualized at that time. Translation: And if the mudabbar's crimes, even if consecutive, only impose one single value, because the master's retention is in one single slave body, and because giving the value is like giving the slave, and that (giving the slave) is not repeated, so likewise (giving the value is not repeated). And they (the victims) become partners in the value according to their shares, and for each one, the value of the mudabbar at the time of the crime against him is considered because the retention is actualized at that time. Commentary: If the mudabbar repeatedly commits crimes and offenses, only one value will be obligatory upon the master, because if the criminal were eligible to be handed over, only one handing over would be obligatory. And giving the value is like giving the slave, so only one value becomes obligatory. Then the claimants (victims) will share in that value proportionally, and the value of the mudabbar at the time of each crime is considered for that claimant because the master's retention is found for him at that time. For example, if the mudabbar committed crimes against three persons: Zayd, Khalid, and Sajid. At the time of the crime against Zayd, his value is 100 rupees; at the time of the crime against Khalid, 50; and at the time of the crime against Sajid, 25. Due to price fluctuations, these differences occur. Now, when the master pays a value by order of the judge, they will share according to this calculation. For example, if he pays 75 rupees, then Zayd gets 100/175, Khalid 50/175, Sajid 25/175. In other words, whatever the value, it is divided into seven parts in the mentioned scenario: Zayd gets 4/7, Khalid 2/7, Sajid 1/7. Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage begins with a ruling on the rights of victims when a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after the master's death) commits multiple successive crimes (jinayat). The master's liability is limited to a single payment of the slave's value, regardless of the number of crimes, because the master's right of retention is only over one slave body. This is analogous to handing over the slave himself—just as a slave cannot be handed over multiple times for multiple crimes, the value (qīmah) stands in place of the slave and thus is also paid only once. The victims share proportionally in this single value based on the value of the slave at the time each crime was committed. This ruling sets the general principle. The passage then addresses a specific scenario: if the master has already paid the value for the first crime, either by court order (qadā') or voluntarily, and later the mudabbar commits a second crime. If the payment was made by court order, the master is deemed compelled to pay, and he bears no further liability for the second crime; the second victim has no claim against him. However, if the payment was made voluntarily (without qadā'), there is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilāf). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the second victim has a choice (khiyār): he may either pursue the master for his right or pursue the first victim (who received the payment). Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the master wronged the second victim by voluntarily paying the value to the first victim, thereby prejudicing the second victim's claim; the first victim, in turn, is considered a guarantor (ḍāmin) for having received the payment unjustly. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the master bears no liability because at the time of payment the second crime did not exist, so the master fully discharged his obligation to the rightful recipient. Their view treats the voluntary payment like a court-ordered payment, absolving the master. The text presents both positions without explicit tarjīḥ, though the commentary notes the difference. This passage is an example of how the Hanafi school derives rules through analogy (qiyās) and how differences within the school (ikhtilāf) are resolved by weighing competing analogies and principles.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍā): A court order or judicial decree; payment made by order of the qadi is considered compelled, thus the master is not liable for subsequent crimes.
تخيير (takhyīr): The option or choice given to the second victim under Abu Hanifa's view: he may choose to pursue the master or the first victim.
ضامن (ḍāmin): Guarantor or person liable; here, the first victim becomes a guarantor for the value received unjustly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to Abu Hanifa, if a master voluntarily pays the value for a mudabbar's first crime (without court order), and the mudabbar then commits a second crime, what is the second victim's position?
A) The second victim has no claim against the master; he must claim from the first victim.
B) The second victim may choose to claim from the master or from the first victim.

Key Takeaway
Voluntary payment by the master does not automatically absolve him from liability for a subsequent crime according to Abu Hanifa; the second victim may choose to pursue the master or the first victim.

intermediate

Mudabbar's Second Jinayat: Master's Liability When Paid without Qada
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's liability for second jinayat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's liability for a mudabbar's second jinayat after voluntary payment for the first, and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A master who pays for a mudabbar's first jinayat voluntarily may still be liable for a second because of shared wrongdoing.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability of a master whose mudabbar (a slave with deferred manumission) commits a second homicide after the master has already paid the value for the first. The ruling hinges on whether the payment was made by judicial decree (qada) or voluntarily. We will trace the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, and uncover the sophisticated legal principles that underlie their difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the undisputed ruling
Begin by establishing the baseline rule: when the master pays the first victim's heir by qada (judicial decree), he is compelled to pay and is thus exempt from any claim arising from a subsequent jinayat. No liability attaches to him.
This is agreed upon by all Hanafi authorities.
2
Recognize the point of ikhtilaf
Now consider the case where the master pays the first victim's heir voluntarily, without a qadi's order. If the mudabbar then commits a second jinayat, the master's liability is disputed between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
This is a classic intra-madhhab dispute with practical consequences.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and its proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the second victim's heir may choose to pursue either the master or the first victim's heir. His reasoning: the master wrongfully paid the right of the second heir, and the first heir wrongfully seized it. Both share in the wrongdoing, so the second heir has a choice.
Abu Hanifa's proof is based on istihsan and the concept of shabahayn (dual similarities).
4
State the Sahibayn's position and its proof
The Sahibayn argue that the master owes nothing. At the time of payment, the second jinayat did not exist, so the master paid the full right to its rightful owner. Hence his payment is analogous to payment by qada, which extinguishes further claims.
Their dalil is that the master was a voluntary payer of a right that existed fully at that time.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the second heir can sue either the master or the first heir, giving him more options. Under the Sahibayn, the master is protected, and the second heir can only claim from the first heir.
This ikhtilaf affects litigation strategy and liability allocation.

قال فإن جن جناية أخرى وقد دفع المولى القيمة إلى ولى الجناية بقضاء فلاشيء عليه لأنه مجبور على الدفع
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا پس اگر مدبر نے دوسری جنایت کی حالانکہ مولیٰ جنایت اولی کے ولی کو قاضی کے فیصلہ سے قیمت دے چکا ہے تو اس پر کچھ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ مولیٰ دینے پر مجبور ہے۔
تشریح اگر مد بر نے جنایت کی اور مولی نے اس کی قیمت وتی جنایت کو دیدی اور قاضی کے حکم سے دی اور مد بر نے دوسری جنایت اور کر دی تو اب مولی پر کوئی ضمان نہ ہو گا۔
سوال ... اس پر ضمان ہونا چاہئے کیونکہ اس نے سارا حق ولی جنایت اولی کے حوالہ کر دیا ہے۔ جواب اس نے جو کچھ کیا ہے قاضی کے حکم سے کیا ہے اور قاضی کے حکم کے بعد وہ دینے پر مجبور ہے۔
اگر بغیر قضاء قاضی کے مولیٰ نے قیمت ادا کر دی تو ولی جنایت کو اختیار ہے چاہے مولیٰ کا پیچھا کرے چاہے پہلے والے ولی جنایت کا پیچھا کرے، اقوال فقہاء
قال وإن كان المولى دفع القيمة بغير قضاء فالولى بالخيار إن شاء اتبع المولى وإن شاء اتبع ولى الجناية وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا شيء على المولى لأنه حين دفع لم تكن الجناية الثانية موجودة فقد دفع كل الحق إلى مستحقه وصار كما إذا دفع بالقضاء
ترجمه قدوری نے فرمایا اور اگر مولیٰ نے بغیر قضاء قاضی کے قیمت دیدی ہو تو ولی جنایت کو اختیار ہے اگر چاہے مولی کا پیچھا کرے اور اگر چاہے ( پہلے ) ولی جنایت کا پیچھا کرے اور یہ ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ مولیٰ پر کچھ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ جب اس نے قیمت دی تو دوسری جنایت موجود نہیں تھی تو مولی نے پورا حق اس کے مستحق کی طرف دیدیا اور ایسا ہو گیا جیسے جب کہ اس سے قضاء قاضی سے دیا ہو۔
تشریح اور اگر آقا نے بغیر قاضی کے فیصلہ کے قیمت ادا کر دی ہو پھر مد بر نے دوسری جنایت کر دی تو اب کیا ہوگا ؟ تو صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ یہاں دوسری جنایت کے ولی کو اختیار ہو گا چاہے تو اپنا حق مولیٰ سے طلب کرے اور چاہے تو ولی جنایت اولی سے طلب کرے۔ دلیل صاحبین جب مولی نے ولی جنایت اولی کو قیمت دی تھی اس وقت دوسری جنایت موجود نہیں تھی اور مولی نے مستحق حق کو اس کا پورا پورا حق ادا کر دیا تھا تو اس کا خود ادا کر دینا ایسا ہو گیا جیسے قاضی کے حکم سے دینا۔
امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل
ولأبي حنيفة أن المولى جان بدفع حق ولى الجناية الثانية طوعا وولى الأولى ضامن بقبض حقه ظلما فيتخير
ترجمہ اور ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ مجرم ہے اپنی رضامندی سے جنایت ثانیہ کا حق دینے کی وجہ سے اور جنایت اولی کا ولی ضامن ہے اس کے حق پر ظلما قبضہ کرنے کی وجہ سے پس وہ ( ولی ثانیہ ) مختار ہوگا۔ تشریح یہ امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ جرم میں دونوں کا اشتراک ہے مولیٰ کا بھی اور ولی جنایت کا بھی مولی کا یہ کہ. اس نے اپنی رضا مندی سے دوسرے کا حق اول کے سپرد کر دیا ہے اور ولی جنایت اولی کا جرم یہ ہے کہ اس نے دوسرے کے حق پر نا جائز قبضہ کر لیا لہذا ثانی کو اختیار ملا کہ اول کو پکڑے یا مولی کو مگر امام صاحب کی یہ منطق کچھ عجیب سی ہے اس کی مزید وضاحت کرتے ہیں۔ ثانی ولی جنایت اولی کب شریک ہوتا ہے
وهذا لأن الثانية مقارنة حكما من وجه وهذا يشارك ولى الجناية الاولى ومتأخرة حكما من حيث أنه تعتبر قيمته يوم الجناية الثانية فى حقها فجعلت كالمقارنة فى حق التضمين لإبطاله ما تعلق به من حق ولى الثانية عملا بالشبهين
ترجمہ اور یہ اس لئے کہ جنایت ثانیہ اولیٰ سے حکماً من وجه مقارن ہے اس وجہ سے ثانی ولی جنایت اولیٰ کا شریک ہو جاتا ہے اور ثانیہ اولیٰ سے حکماً مؤخر ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ ثانیہ کے حق میں مدبر کی قیمت وہ معتبر ہوتی ہے جو دوسری جنایت کے روز تھی تو تضمین کے حق میں ثانیہ کو متصل کے مثل قرار دیا جائے گا۔ مولی کے باطل کرنے کی وجہ سے اس ولی ثانیہ کے حق کو حمد تر سے متعلق تھا دونوں مشابہتوں پر عمل کرتے ہوئے۔
تشریح عجیب ہی منطق کا حل پیش فرما رہے ہیں کہ دوسری جنایت کے دو پہلو ہیں منصوبہ وہ اولی سے مؤخر ہے اور من وجہ مقارن اور متصل ہے اس مقارنت کی وجہ سے بہت سی صورتوں میں ثانی بھی اول کا شریک ہو کہ مدبر میں حصہ پاتا ہے اور مؤخر ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ عافی جنایت میں وہ قیمت معتبر ہوگی جو دوسری جنایت کے دن مدیر کی تھی، بہر حال جب ثانی کے یہ دو پہلو تھے تو ہم نے دونوں مشابہتوں پر عمل کی غرض سے یہ کیا کہ تضمین کے حق میں ہم نے اس کو مقارن و متصل کا مثل سمجھ کر منمان واجب کر دیا اور چونکہ اس کا بھی حق مد بر کے ساتھ وابستہ تھا جس میں مولیٰ اور ولی اولی دونوں کا قصور ہے اس وجہ سے دونوں کا پیچھا کرنے کا حق دیا گیا ہے۔ اور دونوں مشابہتوں پر عمل اس طرح ہوا کہ منمان واجب کر دیا اتصال کی وجہ سے لیکن اس کی قیمت گھٹا کر وہ معتبر ہوئی جو اس دن اس کی قیمت تھی یا مؤخر ہونے کی وجہ سے ہوا ہے نتائج الافکار ۳۷۷ پر یہاں بہت بحث ہے۔
Book of Diyat. The mudabbar committed a second jinayat while the master had already paid the value to the first victim's heir by the qadi's judgment, so nothing is due from the master because he was compelled to pay. The Quduri said: If the mudabbar commits another jinayat and the master has paid the value to the first victim's heir by qada, nothing is due from him because he was forced to pay. Explanation: If the mudabbar committed a jinayat and the master paid its value to the first victim's heir by the qadi's order, and then the mudabbar committed a second jinayat, no daman is due from the master. Question: Should daman be due because he transferred all rights to the first heir? Answer: He did it by qadi's order and after that he was compelled to pay. If the master paid the value without qada, the victim's heir has a choice: if he wishes he may pursue the master, if he wishes he may pursue the first victim's heir. This is the saying of the fuqaha. The Quduri said: If the master paid the value without qada, the victim's heir has a choice; if he wishes he may pursue the master, if he wishes he may pursue the first victim's heir. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: Nothing is due from the master because when he paid, the second jinayat did not exist; he paid the entire right to its rightful owner, and it is as if he paid by qada. Explanation: If the master paid without qadi's judgment and then the mudabbar committed a second jinayat, what happens? The two said: The second victim's heir has a choice to claim his right from the master or from the first victim's heir. The proof of the two: When the master paid the first heir, the second jinayat did not exist, and he paid the full right to its rightful owner, so his payment is like payment by qada. Proof of Abu Hanifa: The master is a wrongdoer by voluntarily paying the right of the second victim's heir, and the first heir is a guarantor by wrongfully seizing his right, so the second heir has a choice. Explanation: This is the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa. It means that both share in the crime: the master voluntarily surrendered another's right to the first, and the first wrongfully seized another's right; therefore the second heir gets the option to pursue either. Then further clarification: The second jinayat is legally concurrent with the first in one respect and delayed in another. The second shares with the first heir due to the concurrency; it is delayed in that the value of the mudabbar is assessed on the day of the second jinayat. Thus in the matter of daman, it is treated as concurrent because the master invalidated the right of the second heir by his payment. Acting on both similarities, daman is imposed on the master, but the value is reduced to that of the day of the second jinayat due to the aspect of delay.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya's commentary on the Kitab al-Diyat discusses the liability of a master when his mudabbar (a slave who will be freed upon the master's death) commits multiple jinayat (homicidal offenses). The ruling differentiates based on whether the master paid the first victim's heir by judicial decree (qada) or voluntarily. If he paid by qada, he is exempt from the second claim because he was compelled. If he paid without qada, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the second victim's heir can choose to pursue either the master or the first heir, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that the master is free because he paid the full right to its rightful owner at a time when the second jinayat did not exist. The commentary then explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning that both the master and the first heir share in the wrongdoing, so the second heir has a choice. Finally, it provides a more detailed juristic analysis: the second jinayat is considered legally concurrent (muqarin) in one aspect (so the second heir shares in the mudabbar's value) and delayed (mutakhkhir) in another (so the value is assessed at the time of the second jinayat). Acting on both aspects, the master remains liable, but the value is the lower one. This is a sophisticated application of istihsan based on shabahayn (two similarities).

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has declared to be free upon his death (tadbir).
جنایت (jinayat): Homicidal offense or injury that requires monetary compensation (diyah or qimah).
قضاء (qada): Judicial decree or ruling of a qadi; payment made under qada is binding and extinguishes liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the master not liable for the second jinayat when he paid the first voluntarily?
A) Because the master was compelled to pay.
B) Because the second jinayat did not exist at the time of payment.
C) Because the first victim's heir waived his right.
D) Because the mudabbar is not liable for multiple jinayat.

Key Takeaway
The master's liability for a mudabbar's subsequent jinayat after voluntary payment is contested: Abu Hanifa grants the second heir a choice, while the Sahibayn exempt the master based on the nonexistence of the second claim at the time of payment.

Only One Qimah Due if Mudabbar Freed After Multiple Jinayat
Ruling: only one qimah for multiple jinayat of mudabbar freed by master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master frees a mudabbar after multiple jinayat and apply the same ruling to an umm al-walad.

Core Idea
Multiple jinayat from a mudabbar require only one qimah because liability arises from the single act of man' (withholding).

This lesson examines a concise ruling from the Hanafi matn: if a mudabbar commits several crimes (jinayat) and the master subsequently frees him, the master's liability is limited to paying one market value (qimah). The underlying principle is that the master's responsibility stems from his act of preventing the slave from being sold to compensate victims, and that prevention is a single event. The same rule applies to an umm al-walad because her status also prevents sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The master frees the mudabbar after the slave has committed multiple jinayat. The ruling: only one qimah (value) is due from the master.
This is the core statement to memorize.
2
Understand the legal cause (illah)
Liability is based on the master's act of man' (withholding the slave from the victims), not on each individual jinayat. Man' is a single act.
If the master had surrendered the slave, no liability would attach.
3
Note the implication of freeing after jinayat
Freeing the slave after the jinayat does not increase the liability; it is as if the freeing never happened in terms of the number of obligations.
The liability crystallizes at the moment of man'.
4
Apply the analogy to umm al-walad
The same ruling applies to an umm al-walad because her status (istilad) also prevents her delivery for sale, just like tadbir.
The reasoning is identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أعتق المولى المدبر وقد جنى جنايات لم تلزمه إلا قيمة واحدة لأن الضمان إنما وجب عليه بالمنع فصار وجود الإعتاق من بعد وعدمه بمنزلة وأم الولد بمنزلة المدير فى جميع ما وصفنا لأن الاستيلاد مانع من الدفع كالتدبير
ترجمہ اور جب مولی نے مد برکو آزاد کردیا حالانکہ اس نے بہت سی جنایات کر رکھی ہیں تو مولی پر ایک ہی قیمت واجب ہوگی اس لئے کہ منمان روکنے کی وجہ سے واجب ہوتا ہے ( تدبیر کے سبب ) تو اس کے بعد اعتناق کا پایا جانا اور اس کا عدم ایک ہی درجہ میں ہیں اور ان
And when the master frees the mudabbar while he has committed jinayat, nothing is binding upon him except one value, because daman is only wajib upon him due to man' (preventing the slave's delivery). So the occurrence of i'taq (freeing) after that and its non-occurrence are alike. And umm al-walad is like the mudabbar in all that we have described because istilad prevents delivery just like tadbir.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This is a short ruling from the matn (likely Al-Quduri) stating that if a master frees his mudabbar after the slave has committed multiple jinayat, the master is only liable for one qimah (value) of the slave. The reason is that the master's liability for the jinayat of a mudabbar is based on his withholding (man') the slave from the victims; he could have surrendered the slave to be sold for compensation but instead prevented that. This liability is a single obligation that attaches to the master's right to the slave's value. Freeing the slave after the jinayat does not increase the liability; it is as if the freeing never happened in terms of the number of obligations. The passage also notes that the same ruling applies to an umm walad (a slave who bears her master's child) because her status also prevents her delivery for sale, similar to tadbir.

Key Terms
قیمت (qimah): The market value of the mudabbar slave, which the master must pay when he withholds the slave from being sold to satisfy the victims' claims.
منع (man'): Prevention or withholding; the master's act of not delivering the slave to be sold for compensation.
ام الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master and cannot be sold or transferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master frees his mudabbar who has committed three separate jinayat, how many qimah (value) is the master liable to pay?
A) Three qimah, one for each jinayat.
B) One qimah only.
C) Half a qimah.
D) No qimah because the slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
When a master frees a mudabbar after multiple jinayat, only one qimah is due because liability arises from the single act of man' (withholding), not from each jinayat.

Master's Single Liability for Multiple Jinayat of Mudabbir
Ruling with proof by talil: single value liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a master owes only one value for multiple jinayat of a mudabbir and explain the reasoning (talil) based on prevention (man').

Core Idea
Only one value is due from the master for multiple injuries of a mudabbir because liability arises from preventing delivery, not from each act.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a mudabbir slave commits multiple acts of injury, and then the master frees him. How many payments does the master owe? The answer clarifies a key Hanafi principle about liability in jinayat: the master's obligation is tied to the single value of the slave, not the number of injuries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage to find the core ruling: when the master frees the mudabbir after multiple jinayat, only one value (qimah) is due from the master.
The ruling applies regardless of whether the master frees the slave after the injuries.
2
Locate the reasoning (talil)
The text states: 'because the guarantee became incumbent upon him due to prevention (of delivery).' Understand that the master's liability stems from his preventing the victims from accessing the slave, not from the injuries themselves.
'Man'' is the legal cause (illah).
3
Analyze the equivalency
The passage says that emancipation afterwards and its non-occurrence are equivalent – meaning freeing the slave does not change the liability already fixed.
The liability is established at the time of the injuries, before emancipation.
4
Apply analogy to umm walad
The same rule applies to an umm walad (slave mother of the master's child) because she also cannot be delivered (sold or transferred) due to her status.
Both mudabbir and umm walad are 'man' (prevented from delivery) in the master's lifetime.
5
Connect to broader principle
This illustrates a broader Hanafi principle: when liability is based on a single asset (the slave's value), multiple claims do not multiply the asset; the value is paid once and distributed among victims pro rata.
This is consistent with the Hanafi treatment of the 'jani' (offender) as an object of value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أعتق المولى المدبر وقد جنى جنايات لم تلزمه إلا قيمة واحدة لأن الضمان إنما وجب عليه بالمنع فصار وجود الإعتاق من بعد وعدمه بمنزلة وأم الولد بمنزلة المدير فى جميع ما وصفنا لأن الاستيلاد مانع من الدفع كالتدبير
ترجمہ اور جب مولی نے مدبر کو آزاد کردیا حالانکہ اس نے بہت سی جنایات کر رکھی ہیں تو مولی پر ایک ہی قیمت واجب ہوگی اس لئے کہ منمان روکنے کی وجہ سے واجب ہوتا ہے ( تدبیر کے سبب ) تو اس کے بعد اعتناق کا پایا جانا اور اس کا عدم ایک ہی درجہ میں ہیں اور ان تمام احکام میں جو ہم نے بیان کئے ہیں ام ولد مدبر کے درجہ میں ہے اس لئے کہ ام ولد بنانا اس کے دینے مد بیر کے مثل مانع ہے۔
And when the master frees the mudabbir while he has committed multiple jinayat, only one value is incumbent upon him because the guarantee became incumbent upon him due to prevention (of delivery). So the occurrence of emancipation afterwards and its non-occurrence are equivalent. And umm walad is like the mudabbir in all that we have described because being an umm walad is a hindrance to delivery like tadbir.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Hanafi fiqh regarding the liability of a master when his mudabbir slave commits multiple acts of injury (jinayat). The key point: only one payment of the slave's value is due from the master, regardless of the number of injuries. The reasoning (talil) is that the master's liability arises from his preventing the delivery of the slave to satisfy the victims (man'). Since the master prevented delivery (due to the slavery relationship), he is liable. Freeing the slave after the injuries does not change this because the liability was already established. The status of umm walad (the mother of the master's child) is analogous: she cannot be delivered either, so the same rule applies. The commentary clarifies that the master's obligation is single because it stems from the restriction of the slave's accessibility, not from the individual acts.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whose freedom is contingent upon the master's death (tadbir).
جنایت (jinayah): Any act causing bodily harm or death; here, it refers to injuries (not necessarily intentional).
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child from her master, and is thus freed upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudabbir commits three acts of unintentional injury (jinayat khata') and then the master frees him, how many payments of the slave's value are due from the master?
A) Three payments
B) One payment
C) No payment
D) Payment equal to the sum of the three injuries

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that only one value is due for multiple jinayat of a mudabbir rests on the principle that liability arises from the prevention of delivery, not the number of acts.

Mudabbir's Confession of Accidental Killing Invalid Against Master
Stating that mudabbir's confession of accidental killing does not bind the master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a mudabbir’s confession of accidental killing and explain why it does not bind the master.

Core Idea
A slave's confession cannot impose financial liability on the master.

In Hanafi law, when a slave commits an accidental injury or killing, the blood money (diyah) falls on the master as the slave cannot own property. Consequently, a confession by the slave regarding such an act cannot be effective against the master. This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies that even if the slave is a mudabbir (promised freedom upon the master's death), his confession remains invalid and imposes no obligation on the master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a mudabbir's confession (iqrar) of accidental killing (jinaayah al-khata'). The ruling is that this confession is not valid (la yajuz) and does not make anything incumbent on the master.
The confession itself is invalid (batil), not merely unenforceable.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is the principle that the consequence (muwjab) of accidental killing—the blood money—is upon the master. The slave's confession cannot override this responsibility because it seeks to impose a liability on the master without his consent.
The dalil is based on the slave's lack of capacity to bind the master financially.
3
Trace the legal link (wajh al-dalalah)
Since a slave cannot own property, any financial obligation from his actions (like diyah) is borne by the master. A confession is an acknowledgment of liability, but the slave has no authority to create liability for the master. Therefore, the confession has no effect.
This reasoning applies whether the slave has been freed or not, because the event occurred during slavery.
4
Note the scope and exceptions
The ruling applies to all slaves (including mudabbir). If the confession were for an intentional killing, the liability may fall on the slave himself if he possesses property; otherwise, the master may choose to pay ransom. For accidental killing, the master's liability is direct, so the confession is especially ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أقر المدبر بجناية الخطأ لم يجز إقراره ولا يلزمه به شيء عتق أو لم يعتق لأن موجب جناية الخطأ على سيده وإقراره به لاينفذ على السيد . والله أعلم
ترجمہ اور جب مد بر نے خطاء جنایت کا اقرار کیا تو اس کا اقرار جائز نہیں ہے اور مولی پر اس کے اقرار کی وجہ سے کچھ لازم نہ ہو گا وہ آزاد کیا جا چکا ہو یا نہ کیا گیا ہو اس لئے کہ خطاء جنایت کا موجب اس کے آقا پر ہے اور ضمان کے بارے میں مدبر کا اقرار آقا پر نافذ نہ ہو گا ، واللہ اعلم .
And when the mudabbir confesses to an accidental injury (jinaayah al-khata'), his confession is not valid and nothing becomes incumbent upon him due to it, whether he has been freed or not, because the consequence of accidental injury is upon his master, and his confession is not effective against the master. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding the mudabbir's confession of an accidental killing or injury. In Hanafi fiqh, the blood money (diyah) for accidental causing of death or injury done by a slave is the responsibility of the master (aqlah). Since the slave cannot own property, any financial obligation from his actions falls on the master. Therefore, a confession by the mudabbir, which would normally establish liability, is not valid because it seeks to impose a financial duty on the master, and the slave cannot act against the master's interests. This applies whether the mudabbir is still a mudabbir or has already been freed, because the confession pertains to an event that occurred during the period of slavery. The Urdu commentary elaborates that the master is not bound by the slave's confession regarding such matters.

Key Terms
جنایت خطاء (jinaayah al-khata'): An unintentional act causing injury or death; accidental homicide or injury.
اقرار (iqrar): A legal confession or acknowledgment of a right or liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The confession is valid and the master must pay the blood money.
B) The confession is invalid and the master owes nothing as a result of the confession.
C) The confession is valid but the master may disown it.
D) The confession is invalid only if the master objects.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession of accidental killing is legally invalid and cannot impose any financial liability on the master.
